<div class="grid-container pmc-actions-bar" aria-label="Actions bar" role="complementary">
    <div class="grid-row">
        <div class="grid-col-fill display-flex">
             <div class="display-flex">

<hgroup><h1>Mimicking the host and its microenvironment <em>in vitro</em> for studying mucosal infections by <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em>
</h1></hgroup><div class="cg p">
<a href="https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/?term=%22Crabb%C3%A9%20A%22%5BAuthor%5D" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="id1"><span class="name western">Aurélie Crabbé</span></a><div hidden="hidden" id="id1">

<div class="p">

<sup>1,</sup><sup>*</sup>, <a href="https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/?term=%22Ledesma%20MA%22%5BAuthor%5D" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="id2"><span class="name western">Maria A Ledesma</span></a><div hidden="hidden" id="id2">

<div class="ra xbox p" role="complementary" aria-label="Related or updated information about this article"><div>The publisher's version of this article is available at <a href="https://doi.org/10.1111/2049-632X.12180" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Pathog Dis</a>
</div></div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="P1">Why is a healthy person protected from <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em> infections, while individuals with cystic fibrosis or damaged epithelium are particularly susceptible to this opportunistic pathogen? In order to address this question, it is essential to thoroughly understand the dynamic interplay between the host microenvironment and <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. Therefore, using modeI systems that represent key aspects of human mucosal tissues in health and disease allows recreating <em>in vivo</em> host-pathogen interactions in a physiologically relevant manner. In this review, we discuss how factors of mucosal tissues, such as apical-basolateral polarity, junctional complexes, extracellular matrix proteins, mucus, multicellular complexity (including indigenous microbiota), and other physicochemical factors affect <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis and are thus important to mimic <em>in vitro</em>. We highlight <em>in vitro</em> cell and tissue culture model systems of increasing complexity that have been used over the past 35 years to study the infectious disease process of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, mainly focusing on lung models, and their respective advantages and limitations. Continued improvements of <em>in vitro</em> models based on our expanding knowledge of host microenvironmental factors that participate in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis will help advance fundamental understanding of pathogenic mechanisms and increase the translational potential of research findings from bench to the patient’s bedside.</p></section><section id="S1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p id="P2">As a leading cause of life-threatening nosocomial infections, <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em> has been extensively studied over the past 35 years. No currently available <em>in vitro</em> or <em>in vivo</em> model system is able to completely mimic the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> disease phenotype. Accordingly, the complexity of the host, pathogen and host-pathogen interactions has necessitated a combined use of different model systems to advance our understanding of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease. <em>P. aeruginosa</em> is most commonly studied in the context of cystic fibrosis (CF) patients, where chronic lung inflammation caused by this microorganism is believed to be the major source of mortality (<a href="#R217" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R217">Yang, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R43">Doring, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). In the thick, viscous CF lung mucus, <em>P. aeruginosa</em> primarily exists as persistent alginate-overproducing (mucoid) biofilms that adhere to mucus compounds, but not to lung epithelium, and are resistant to available antimicrobial agents and the host immune system (<a href="#R89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R89">Hoiby, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R179">Schobert &amp; Tielen, 2010</a>, <a href="#R19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R19">Breidenstein, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). <em>P. aeruginosa</em>-associated components (such as LPS, lectins, alginate) and secreted factors (such as quorum sensing molecules and pyocyanin) generate an extreme inflammatory response, which is destructive for the host but not the pathogen (<a href="#R66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R66">Gellatly &amp; Hancock, 2013</a>). Furthermore, acute <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections frequently occur in patient populations with a damaged epithelial barrier (such as in burn wound patients, following intestinal surgery, at insertion sites of catheters or endotracheal tubes, or in scratched cornea), or compromised immune system (such as HIV and cancer patients) (<a href="#R66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R66">Gellatly &amp; Hancock, 2013</a>). During acute infections, disease or injury-related epithelial modifications that mediate <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion are believed to contribute to the infectious disease process, thereby necessitating direct host-pathogen interactions (<a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>). Herewith, the type three secretion system (T3SS) (<a href="#R112" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R112">Lee, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>), various adhesins (such as lectins, flagella and type IV pili) and secreted virulence factors dictate the course of disease (<a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>).</p>
<p id="P3"><em>In vitro</em> cell and tissue culture models have served as useful platforms to dissect the molecular mechanisms and phenotypic properties from both the host and pathogen side that underly the infectious disease process. While many <em>in vitro</em> models are oversimplistic and lack key properties of the parental tissue, increasing efforts are made to design more physiologically relevant models, and to explore their potential for <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis studies. At the other extreme are animal models, which have advanced our knowledge on various <em>P. aeruginosa</em>-induced illnesses, but often do not mimic human physiology and disease, are complex and require extensive expertise. Since many <em>in vitro</em> findings have been confirmed using animal models (<a href="#R154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R154">Pier, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R127" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R127">Martin, et al.</a>, <a href="#R22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R22">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>, <a href="#R31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R31">Cory, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>), it is relevant to utilize <em>in vitro</em> model systems to initially explore fundamental questions and to verify select findings <em>in vivo</em>. This approach can significantly <em>refine</em> and <em>reduce</em> the use of animal models. Finally, with promising ongoing projects, such as Donald Ingber’s human-on-a-chip (<a href="#R129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R129">Marx, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>), complex multi-organ systems might one day be engineered which could accomplish the third “R”, <em>replacement</em> of animal models.</p>
<p id="P4">The host microenvironment affects virulence properties of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. Therefore, mimicking the host microenvironment is essential for the development of <em>in vitro</em> infection models that better mimic <em>in vivo</em> phenotypes of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, which will facilitate the design of novel efficacious antimicrobial treatments. In this review, we highlight that the choice of the model system, and the host characteristics that it mimics, can significantly impact the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> phenotype and outcome of the infection study. For detailed information about the immunomodulatory, cytotoxic and detrimental effects that <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and its virulence factors exert on the host during acute and chronic infections, we refer readers to excellent reviews (<a href="#R173" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R173">Sadikot, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R66">Gellatly &amp; Hancock, 2013</a>). This review describes <em>in vitro</em> model systems of mucosal tissues with increasing complexity that reconcile key characteristics of the host involved in the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process. However, before discussing these models and the <em>in vivo</em> traits that they mimic, we start by providing a concise overview of host factors that affect <em>P. aeruginosa</em> disease initiation and progression, regardless of whether they are modified by <em>P. aeruginosa</em> upon host encounter. Given that most <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections are located in mucosal tissues, we focus on this infection site, with particular emphasis on the lung.</p></section><section id="S2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">How does the mucosal epithelium contribute to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence?</h2>
<section id="S3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The healthy mucosal epithelium is protected from <em>P. aeruginosa</em>
</h3>
<p id="P5">The first line of defense against mucosal pathogens like <em>P. aeruginosa</em> relies on physical, chemical and cellular factors. Key host factors that protect the lung from <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection and are altered during disease are depicted in <a href="#F1" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>. While additional host factors are altered during disease, due to space limitations, only those that have a well-documented effect on <em>P. aeruginosa</em> lung pathogenesis are presented. <strong>Physical factors</strong> include sealing of the epithelial paracellular pathway by tight junctions, subjacent adherens junctions, and desmosomes (<a href="#R126" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R126">Marchiando, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). In addition, the mucociliary escalator (with associated fluid shear levels (<a href="#R16" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R16">Blake, 1973</a>, <a href="#R193" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R193">Tarran, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>)) in the mucous lining of the trachea, bronchi and bronchioles captures and subsequently clears more than 90% of inhaled particles (<a href="#R163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R163">Quinton, 2010</a>). Specifically, the biphasic mucus layer covering the airway epithelium enables continuous beating of epithelium-associated cilia, which moves the upper mucus layer towards the laryngopharynx. Besides the binding of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> to mucus components and subsequent removal, mucus contains antimicrobial substances, such as β-defensins and lactoferrins, which are part of the <strong>chemical factors</strong> that keep our lungs and other mucosal surfaces clear of infection by this microorganism (<a href="#R200" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R200">Venkatakrishnan, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Furthermore, chemical factors such as normoxia, low iron levels, neutral pH, bicarbonate and phosphate levels, all contribute to keeping <em>P. aeruginosa</em> to a manageable phenotype (<a href="#R220" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R220">Zaborina, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>, <a href="#R122" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R122">Long, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R171">Romanowski, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R219" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R219">Zaborin, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R8">Babrowski, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>, <a href="#R29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R29">Cornelis &amp; Dingemans, 2013</a>), that can easily be cleared by the innate immune system. In addition to the basal level of innate immune cells such as macrophages present in healthy mucosal tissues, epithelial cells play a key role in the direct defense against <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and in the controlled recruitment of neutrophils for effective eradication of this microorganism. The <strong>cellular</strong> receptor CFTR (cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator) has been shown to bind <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the healthy host, which results in bacterial internalization, apoptosis/exfoliation of the infected cell, and recruitment of phagocytes (reviewed in (<a href="#R152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R152">Pier, 2012</a>)). On the other hand, additional host receptors that bind <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and mediate invasion, such as asialo ganglioside M1 (asialoGM1), and α5β1 integrins, are expressed at basal levels on the apical membrane of polarized epithelial cells (<a href="#R39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R39">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R40">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R41">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). In healthy mucosal epithelium, the basolateral surface is not exposed, which prevents binding to basolateral cell membrane receptors and extracellular matrix (ECM) proteins (<a href="#R39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R39">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Furthermore, epithelial cells can produce enzymatic factors, such as paraoxonases that degrade <em>P. aeruginosa</em> quorum sensing molecules (<a href="#R27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R27">Chun, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>, <a href="#R146" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R146">Ozer, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Finally, the indigenous microbiota of mucosal tissues such as lung and intestine plays an essential role in host defense against <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, through competition for nutrients, activation of innate immune cells and epithelial cell differentiation (including tight junctions) (<a href="#R75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R75">Han, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R123">Lozupone, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R52">Eloe-Fadrosh &amp; Rasko, 2013</a>, <a href="#R73" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R73">Guzman, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Collectively, these physical, chemical and cellular factors of mucosal tissues protect the healthy host from <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F1"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=4096993_nihms586687f1.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/72ba/4096993/ba4e0783df65/nihms586687f1.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1050" width="698" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p id="P30">The lung mucosal tissue in a healthy person (A), a patient with damaged epithelium (e.g., bronchoscopy, cathether, and surgery) (B), and a cystic fibrosis patient (C). Key factors that protect a healthy host against <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection (A) or that contribute to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> initiation of infectious disease (B and C) are depicted. While additional host factors are altered during disease, only those that have a well-documented effect on <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis are presented. In addition, host factors that are changed due to infection by <em>P. aeruginosa</em> are not included or discussed.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="S4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The injured mucosal epithelium mediates <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection</h3>
<p id="P6">Epithelial injury in patients undergoing bronchoscopy, intestinal surgery, or other damaging procedures, renders them particularly susceptible to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection (<a href="#F1" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>, <a href="#T1" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>). First, disruption of tight junctions as part of the <strong>physical barrier</strong> enables paracellular migration to basolateral receptors, which is the preferred infection site for this opportunistic pathogen (<a href="#R57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R57">Fleiszig, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>, <a href="#R110" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R110">Kube, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). In addition, binding to ECM proteins of the basement membrane, such as fibronectin, laminin, and collagens, is part of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> colonization and tissue invasion when the epithelial barrier is damaged (<a href="#R195" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R195">Trafny, <em>et al.</em>, 1995</a>, <a href="#R40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R40">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R156" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R156">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Since repairing epithelial cells are not ciliated (<a href="#R50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R50">Dupuit, <em>et al.</em>, 1995</a>, <a href="#R161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R161">Puchelle, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>), local disruption in mucociliary clearance could further contribute to enhanced <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion and ultimately dissemination. Changes in the microenvironment following intestinal surgery have been found to increase virulence characteristics in <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. Specifically, host <strong>chemical factors</strong> altered during stress, such as release of opioids, low phosphate levels or higher pH trigger a lethal phenotype in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R220" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R220">Zaborina, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>, <a href="#R122" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R122">Long, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R171">Romanowski, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R219" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R219">Zaborin, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R8">Babrowski, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Moreover, the monosaccharide content of breastmilk versus formula was found to affect <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence gene expression, which could be correlated with the development of neonatal necrotic enterocolitis depending on the infant’s diet (<a href="#R142" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R142">Nelson, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Hypoxia following stress in intestinal or other mucosal tissues also profoundly affects virulence traits in <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, including the expression of the adhesin PA-I lectin and enhanced binding via exposure of basolateral membranes (<a href="#R57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R57">Fleiszig, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>, <a href="#R107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R107">Kohler, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Finally, the cytokine interferon-gamma (IFN-γ), produced by the inflamed tissues, has been shown to bind the outer membrane protein OprF of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, which induces PA-I lectin expression (<a href="#R216" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R216">Wu, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). <strong>Cellular factors</strong> are also believed to contribute to the susceptibility of damaged and repairing epithelium upon <em>P. aeruginosa</em> encounter. Specifically, basolateral membrane receptors that bind <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (e.g., heparan sulfate chains of proteoglycans) are enriched at the apical side of repairing mucosal tissue (<a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>), and apical receptors/binding sites such as asialoGM1, fibronectin and the integrin α5β1 are transiently enriched at the surface of spreading and repairing respiratory epithelial cells (<a href="#R41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R41">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Finally, repairing epithelium does not express apical CFTR (<a href="#R161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R161">Puchelle, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>), which could impede effective clearance of infected epithelial cells.</p>

<td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<a href="#R154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R154">Pier, <em>et al</em>., 1996</a>, <a href="#R57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R57">Fleiszig, <em>et al</em>., 1997</a>, <a href="#R58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R58">Fleiszig, <em>et al</em>., 1998</a>, <a href="#R79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R79">Heiniger, <em>et al</em>., 2010</a>, <a href="#R152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R152">Pier, 2012</a>)</td>
</tr>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/T1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="TFN1"><p id="P50">CF = cystic fibrosis, NO = nitric oxide, PQS = <em>Pseudomonas</em> quinolone signal, SOD = superoxide dismutase, CFTR = cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator, 3-oxo-C<sub>12</sub>-HSL = <em>N</em>-(3 oxododecanoyl)-L-homoserine lactone</p></div></div></section></section><section id="S5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The CF lung environment is an ideal ecological niche for <em>P. aeruginosa</em>
</h3>
<p id="P7">The predominance of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the CF lung environment is believed to be multifactorial and is mostly related to the loss of a functional CFTR (<a href="#R217" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R217">Yang, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R152">Pier, 2012</a>) (<a href="#F1" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>, <a href="#T1" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>). While ΔF508-CFTR airway epithelial cells have a decreased barrier function (<a href="#R14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R14">Bebok, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R110" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R110">Kube, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R116" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R116">LeSimple, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>), this phenotypic characteristic is not believed to contribute significantly to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection. However, the primary <strong>physical barrier</strong> component that is causative for chronic infections in the CF lung environment is the absent mucociliary clearance, predominantly due to CFTR-mediated imbalances in bicarbonate levels (<a href="#R163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R163">Quinton, 2010</a>, <a href="#R152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R152">Pier, 2012</a>). The resulting dehydrated mucus layer comprised of compact sticky mucins has been shown to cause many of the CF-related phenotypes of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, including loss of motility, mucus-associated biofilm formation, anaerobic metabolism, and resistance to antibiotics and antimicrobial peptides (<a href="#R189" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R189">Sriramulu, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R131">Matsui, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R63">Fung, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Further, lower fluid shear levels that could be present in the CF lung as a consequence of the impacted mucociliary escalator were also found to generate a CF-like <em>P. aeruginosa</em> phenotype, including self-aggregative biofilms, enhanced alginate production, microaerophilic/anaerobic metabolism, and resistance to oxidative stress (<a href="#R34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R34">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R35">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). The <strong>chemical microenvironment</strong> of the CF lung also significantly contributes to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence and persistence. Indeed, the microaerophilic and anaerobic conditions in the CF lung trigger antibiotic resistance, alginate production, and biofilm formation (<a href="#R215" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R215">Worlitzsch, <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>, <a href="#R76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R76">Hassett, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>, <a href="#R180" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R180">Schobert &amp; Jahn, 2010</a>, <a href="#R179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R179">Schobert &amp; Tielen, 2010</a>). Furthermore, high iron levels, specific amino acids, and lower pH have all been related to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence in the CF lung mucus (<a href="#R147" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R147">Palmer, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>, <a href="#R151" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R151">Pezzulo, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R29">Cornelis &amp; Dingemans, 2013</a>). The CF lung environment is also characterized by high levels of reactive oxygen species (ROS), mostly produced by the influx of neutrophils, which has been shown to induce the characteristic mucoid phenotype of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R130">Mathee, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Finally, high protease levels in the CF lung, particularly neutrophil elastase, can degrade host antimicrobial peptides such as defensins and LL-37, which further impedes clearance of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R212" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R212">Weiner, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R221" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R221">Zhang, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R60">Forde, <em>et al.</em>, 2014</a>). The microbiome is becoming increasingly recognized as a major <strong>cellular component</strong> of the CF lung that contributes to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence. Dozens of bacterial taxa (including <em>Prevotella</em>, <em>Veillonella</em> spp.), of which many are oropharyngeal flora, inhabit CF lung mucus (<a href="#R75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R75">Han, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Decreased community diversity is related to more severe lung disease, and the community dynamics depend on CFTR genotype (<a href="#R32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R32">Cox, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). It has been shown that microbial community members in the CF lung, including from the oropharyngeal flora, increase virulence characteristics of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and could contribute to lung disease (<a href="#R48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R48">Duan, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R201" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R201">Venkataraman, <em>et al.</em>, 2014</a>).</p>
<p id="P8">Since host microenvironmental factors have been demonstrated to be key in the initial survival, persistence, and ultimately adaptation of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the lung (<a href="#R217" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R217">Yang, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R59" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R59">Folkesson, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>), mimicking the host and the microenvironment is essential when studying the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process. In this review, we mainly focus on the lung mucosa environment.</p></section></section><section id="S6"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Which <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models are used to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis in mucosal tissues?</h2>
<section id="S7"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Two-dimensional monolayers</h3>
<p id="P9">An overview of <em>in vitro</em> models of human lung mucosa used to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis is presented in <a href="#T2" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>. The <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process is most frequently studied using a single cell type grown on a non-permeable surface (plastic or glass), referred to as two-dimensional (2-D) monolayers. Two-dimensional monolayers of different cell lines have been used for study of lung disease (A549, 16HBE, CFBE) and intestinal disease (HeLa, T84, CaCo2, HT-29) by <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R175">Saiman, <em>et al.</em>, 1990</a>, <a href="#R154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R154">Pier, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R132" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R132">McNamara, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R137">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R177" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R177">Schaible, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>, <a href="#R210" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R210">Wang, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>, <a href="#R211" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R211">Weichert, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). In addition, primary cells recovered from nasal brushing of human volunteers, human lung biopsies, human nasal polyps and mouse lung grown as 2-D monolayers have been used (<a href="#R2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R2">Amitani, <em>et al.</em>, 1991</a>, <a href="#R159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R159">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1991</a>, <a href="#R9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R9">Bajolet-Laudinat, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R42" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R42">de Courcey, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). There are several advantages of using 2-D monolayers as models for infection studies, including that they are (i) cost effective, (ii) require limited equipment and materials, (iii) easy to set-up, maintain, monitor and manipulate, (iv) quickly ready for downstream assays (e.g., confluence is reached in 3–4 days for A549 cell line), and (v) high-throughput (e.g., multi-well assays). When 2-D monolayers of cell lines are used, a well-characterized homogeneous cell population is usually obtained, which results in high experimental reproducibility. On the other hand, 2-D monolayers often lack differentiation characteristics of the <em>in vivo</em> parental tissue, including 3-D architecture, phenotypic characteristics, multicellular complexity, and function. Specifically, most cell lines or primary cells grown as traditional monolayers on impermeable surfaces are not polarized, and thus do not express distinctive apical and basolateral markers (including surface receptors), which are important in studying aspects of the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection process that require cell polarity, including adhesion (<a href="#R25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R25">Carterson, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>). Therefore, 2-D monolayers are limited in predicting the host response to disease and drug effectiveness. An additional limitation of 2-D monolayers is that cells easily detach from their substrate upon infection, and thus long-term studies are limited using this model system (<a href="#R137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R137">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>).</p>
