<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/T2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="TFN2"><p id="P52">TJ = tight junctions, CFTR = cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator, IVOC = <em>in vitro</em> organ culture</p></div></div></section></section><section id="S8"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Between 2D and 3D: Air-liquid interface</h3>
<p id="P10">Considerable knowledge on <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis has been obtained by growing cells on semi-permeable supports or Transwell<sup>®</sup> membranes. In this model system, cells grown on the surface of ECM-coated (mostly collagen) semi-permeable membranes are submerged in culture medium both at their apical and basolateral sides (<a href="#R204" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R204">Villenave, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). For respiratory mucosa, an air-liquid interface (ALI) can be generated upon reaching confluency (evaluated by Trans Epithelial Resistance (TER) measurement) for further maturation by removing medium at the apical cell surface. Primary airway epithelial cells grown as ALI are one of the best characterized model systems of the lung and closely mimic key aspects of the <em>in vivo</em> architecture, cellular composition and physiology of airway epithelium. Specifically, ALI of primary airway cells are pseudostratified, contain a highly differentiated mixed epithelial cell population (including basal, ciliated, and goblet cells), express relevant cilia coverage and beating, mucus production, polarity and tight junctions (<a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R224" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R224">Zulianello, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R204" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R204">Villenave, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). While the overall morphology of ALI strikingly resembles the <em>in vivo</em> tissue, differences in the proportion of the lung epithelial cells have been reported compared to the native organ (<a href="#R51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R51">Dvorak, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R205" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R205">Villenave, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). However, the <em>in vivo</em>-like characteristics (such as polarity and CFTR expression) of epithelial cells grown on permeable supports strongly depend on the cell source used (<a href="#T2" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). Limitations of Transwell model systems include that they are expensive, time-consuming (regarding manipulations and duration of maturation – e.g., 3–4 weeks for primary airway epithelial cells) and have limited high-throughput capabilities. Interestingly, permeable supports are amenable to co-culturing multiple cell types in a physiologically relevant manner, including innate immune cells (<a href="#R82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R82">Higbee, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>, <a href="#R114" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R114">Leonard, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R113" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R113">Lehmann, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>), and inclusion of biomechanical forces (such as air pressure, viscoelastic stretch force and fluid shear) (<a href="#R190" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R190">Swartz, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R94">Huh, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).</p></section><section id="S9"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Three-dimensional cell culture models</h3>
<p id="P11">Three-dimensional (3-D) cell culture models that have been explored for <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection studies include (i) 3-D models generated using the rotating wall vessel (RWV) bioreactor (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>), and (ii) 3-D cell spheroids generated through gentle agitation (<a href="#R215" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R215">Worlitzsch, <em>et al</em>., 2002</a>, <a href="#R197" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R197">Ulrich &amp; Doring, 2004</a>, <a href="#R198" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R198">Ulrich, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). The RWV is an optimized form of suspension culture in which cells are typically grown on the surface of porous ECM-coated microcarrier beads under low fluid shear and gentle mixing conditions. The dynamic culture conditions in the RWV enable cells to differentiate into well-organized 3-D tissue-like structures (termed 3-D aggregates), which replicate many of the <em>in vivo</em> characteristics including apical-basolateral polarity, a single cell layer for lung and intestinal tissues, tight junctions, mucus production, and differentiation into several epithelial cell types (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). These models are amenable to most infectious disease-related assays, with the exception of transcytosis, enable co-culturing of multiple cell types, offer targeted high-throughput analysis and exhibit key <em>in vivo</em>-like responses to pathogens, their toxins, and antimicrobial therapeutics not observed with conventional cell culture models (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R36">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R164" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R164">Radtke, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Disadvantages are that these models are more expensive, labor-intensive, require relatively long incubation times (e.g. 9–12 days for 3-D A549 model) compared to 2-D monolayers, and are currently not amenable to chronic infection studies.</p>
<p id="P12">Another 3-D lung epithelial model is generated through incubation of primary airway epithelial cells under gentle agitation (<a href="#R215" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R215">Worlitzsch, <em>et al</em>., 2002</a>, <a href="#R197" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R197">Ulrich &amp; Doring, 2004</a>, <a href="#R198" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R198">Ulrich, <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>). As a result, cells naturally aggregate and generate sphere-shaped clumps (spheroids) that contain a mixed cell population, including ciliated and mucus-secreting goblet cells. A major advantage of this 3-D cell culture model is its long-term viability and differentiated state (6 months when derived from nasal polyps and 6 weeks when derived from lung tissue). In addition, this model is easy to generate, inexpensive and could allow targeted high-throughput studies. Downsides include the long culture time (4 weeks) and the questionable physiological relevance, with regard to <em>in vivo</em>-like architecture and polarity.</p></section><section id="S10"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">
<em>In vitro</em> organ cultures</h3>
<p id="P13">Earlier studies have used small intact pieces (about 3 mm<sup>2</sup>, 2–3 mm thick) of human or animal tissue to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection. For lung studies, <em>in vitro</em> organ cultures (IVOCs) were generated from adenoids (from adenoid hypertrophy patients) (<a href="#R196" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R196">Tsang, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>), nasal polyps (<a href="#R9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R9">Bajolet-Laudinat, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>), human nasal turbinate tissue, non-transplanted lung tissue from transplant recipients (<a href="#R45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R45">Dowling, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>, <a href="#R47" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R47">Dowling, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>, <a href="#R46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R46">Dowling, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R176">Sajjan, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>), or from dissected mouse tissue (<a href="#R22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R22">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Advantages of this model system are that it enables research in physiologically relevant human/animal tissue that maintains the <em>in vivo</em> structure and integrity. IVOCs closely approximate the <em>in vivo</em> ratio of differentiated lung cell types (including leucocytes) that interact with the native ECM and submucosal tissues in a physiological 3-D environment (<a href="#R135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R135">Middleton, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R176">Sajjan, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>). Moreover, these models secrete mucus and maintain epithelial integrity for 10 and 20 days, respectively (<a href="#R97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R97">Jackson, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R17">Boat, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>). Disadvantages include the low availability of human tissue biopsies, limited life span of cell types within the tissues (such as leucocytes) (<a href="#R56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R56">Farley, <em>et al.</em>, 1986</a>, <a href="#R62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R62">Forsgren, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>), and biological variation. In addition, this model requires specific expertise and is time-consuming. For <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection studies specifically, exfoliation of epithelial cells is already observed after 4h of infection, which limits the usefulness of this model for long-term studies (<a href="#R158" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R158">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1989</a>). Of note, in a similar manner as for Transwells, IVOCs can be grown at an air-liquid interface (<a href="#R176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R176">Sajjan, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>), which more closely simulates the conditions encountered by the respiratory mucosa <em>in vivo</em>, and can result in significant differences in the infectious disease outcome compared to submersion of the apical surface in medium (<a href="#R135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R135">Middleton, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>).</p></section><section id="S11"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Other cell culture models with potential for <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis</h3>
<p id="P14">In this section, we briefly address other cell culture models that have, to our knowledge, not yet been utilized for studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis in mucosal tissues, but could be of interest. Mucosal cell culture models with <em>in vivo</em>-like 3-D architecture, polarity and other physiological traits are commonly generated by growing cells in a 3-D matrix scaffold comprised of single or multiple ECMs, termed <strong>hydrogels</strong>, that mimic the lamina propria (<a href="#R105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R105">Kleinman &amp; Martin, 2005</a>, <a href="#R96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R96">Inman &amp; Bissell, 2010</a>, <a href="#R222" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R222">Zhang, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Since bacteria will encounter the hydrogel first, followed by the basolateral cell surface, this model is relevant when studying basolateral <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections (<a href="#R1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R1">Alarcon, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>, <a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). For mucosal tissues (where pathogens infect at the apical side), the infection process might not be physiologically relevant (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). <strong>Acellular (or decellularized) scaffolds</strong> generated from animal or human tissues have recently emerged that mimic the complex 3-D architecture and ECM composition of their parental counterpart (reviewed by (<a href="#R207" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R207">Wagner, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>)). Exponential progress has been made over the past years to improve tissue decellularization and repopulation with different cell types (<a href="#R30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R30">Cortiella, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R145" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R145">Ott, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R38" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R38">Daly, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R150" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R150">Petersen, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R37">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2014</a>, <a href="#R134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R134">Mendez, <em>et al.</em>, 2014</a>, <a href="#R208" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R208">Wagner, <em>et al.</em>, 2014</a>). However, complete recellularization of the lung surface with differentiated functional lung epithelium (and establishment of barrier function) has to precede the usefulness of these models for infectious disease research purposes. In the future, model systems generated from decellularized scaffolds might offer attractive platforms to study infectious diseases, given the enhanced 3-D architectural complexity compared to currently available models and the possibility to include biomechanical forces, such as breathing movement. The recently developed <strong>organ-on-a-chip</strong> model simulates key characteristics of the host microenvironment, including biomechanical forces (fluid shear and viscoelastic stretch), 3-D organization of distinct tissues (e.g. interfaces between epithelium and vascular endothelium), and biochemical properties (<a href="#R93" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R93">Huh, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). For example, the lung-on-a-chip model reconstitutes the surfactant-producing alveolar-capillary interface on a collagen-coated porous silicon wafer, and mimics the cyclic biomechanical stretch force of breathing and vascular perfusion (<a href="#R94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R94">Huh, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Neutrophil transmigration in response to apical exposure with <em>Escherichia coli</em> was demonstrated, indicating the usefulness of this system to mimic the innate cellular response to bacterial infection (<a href="#R94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R94">Huh, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Therefore, the organ-on-a-chip model holds tremendous potential for further unraveling of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis mechanisms.</p></section></section><section id="S12"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Which physiological characteristics of mucosal tissue models affect the study of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis?</h2>
<section id="S13"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Apical and basolateral polarity</h3>
<p id="P15">Receptors and other host-associated factors differentially expressed at apical and basolateral surfaces are important for the clearance of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the healthy host, and for colonization of damaged epithelium (reviewed in (<a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>, <a href="#R152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R152">Pier, 2012</a>)). Therefore, mucosal cell culture models that replicate epithelial cell polarity of the host tissue in health and disease are essential to assess host and pathogen responses to infection (<a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>, <a href="#R188" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R188">Srikanth, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R111">Law, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). When studying resistance of the healthy host to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection, it is relevant to mimic the <em>in vivo</em>-like cell polarity (<a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>). On the other hand, it is key to replicate the affected polarity of damaged epithelium when studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the context of acute infections (<a href="#R58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R58">Fleiszig, <em>et al.</em>, 1998</a>, <a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R101">Kazmierczak, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>). When the basolateral epithelial cell surface is accessible in the context of damaged epithelium, it is the preferred site for <em>P. aeruginosa</em> binding (<a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R21">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Since basolateral receptors are enriched at the apical side of repairing (incompletely polarized) epithelium, <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion, invasion, and cytotoxicity is enhanced compared to polarized epithelium (<a href="#R79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R79">Heiniger, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>) (<a href="#R58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R58">Fleiszig, <em>et al.</em>, 1998</a>, <a href="#R101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R101">Kazmierczak, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>). Interestingly, <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (both planktonic cells and aggregates) can enhance the expression of basolateral receptors at the apical surface to facilitate its uptake through activation of host signaling pathways (<a href="#R102" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R102">Kierbel, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R65">Gassama-Diagne, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R103">Kierbel, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>, <a href="#R115" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R115">Lepanto, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R21">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). In addition, putative <em>P. aeruginosa</em> receptors such as apical asialo ganglioside M1 (asialoGM1), and the integrin α5β1 are transiently enriched at the surface of spreading and repairing respiratory epithelial cells (<a href="#R39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R39">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>), even though the role of asialoGM1 in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> binding is controversial (<a href="#R181" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R181">Schroeder, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>). Furthermore, apical expression of N-glycoproteins depends on the differentiation/polarization status of epithelial cells, and affects type IV pili-mediated binding, invasion and cytotoxicity of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R21">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Therefore, binding of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> to apical surfaces can be inhibited by competitive blockage of exogenous sugars (<a href="#R22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R22">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). To further validate the role of cell polarity during <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections, adhesion and invasion of this bacterium was significantly reduced in a polarized 3-D A549 lung model compared to non-polarized 2-D A549 monolayers (<a href="#R25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R25">Carterson, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Differential colonization of polarized and non-polarized epithelium is also observed for other mucosal pathogens (such as <em>Salmonella</em>) (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R111">Law, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>).</p>
<p id="P16">A host cell receptor that could be implicated in the clearance of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> from the healthy host is CFTR (<a href="#R154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R154">Pier, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R154">Pier, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>), even though controversies exist (<a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Specifically, CFTR binds the outer LPS core of non-mucoid <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and initiates a swift, coordinated inflammatory response (through NF-κB) that results in pathogen removal via recruitment of innate immune cells (<em>in vivo</em>) and apoptosis of the infected cells (<em>in vitro</em>) (<a href="#R182" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R182">Schroeder, <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>). When studying the role of CFTR in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> colonization, it is important to consider that CFTR trafficking, apical expression and functionality depends on cell polarity for many cell lines (e.g., HT-29, 16 HBE) (<a href="#R140" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R140">Morris, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R121" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R121">Loffing-Cueni, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>). Indeed, wild type bronchial epithelial cells grown as non-polarized 2-D monolayers express low levels of CFTR (<a href="#R153" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R153">Pier, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>), and the functionality of CFTR has been shown to improve when cells are grown as ALI (<a href="#R13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R13">Bebok, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>).</p>
<p id="P17">Taken together, the loss of polarity in repairing epithelium facilitates <em>P. aeruginosa</em> colonization compared to intact epithelium, presumably through increased expression of apical receptors, enhanced binding to basolateral receptors, and absence of a functional CFTR. While these studies are relevant for acute infections involving damaged epithelium, they highlight that tissue culture models of improperly polarized epithelium can result in an artificially different susceptibility to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection (<a href="#R25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R25">Carterson, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R79">Heiniger, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).</p></section><section id="S14"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Junctional complexes</h3>
<p id="P18">When studying the influence of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and its virulence factors on epithelial barrier integrity or other aspects of the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process, choosing a cell culture model that expresses markers of the apical junctional complex is critical. While most epithelial cells, including lung, require growth on permeable membranes or as 3-D cultures to form tight junctions (e.g. A549, ΔF508-CFTR and WT-CFTR bronchial epithelial cells), a limited number of epithelial cells are capable of forming tight junctions when grown as 2-D monolayers (e.g., Caco-2, HT-29, T84) (<a href="#R98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R98">Jain, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R86">Hoentsch, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R111">Law, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Interestingly, certain epithelial cells show enhanced barrier function in response to lipoxins, azithromycin or hypoxia (<a href="#R107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R107">Kohler, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R6">Asgrimsson, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R72">Grumbach, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>, <a href="#R85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R85">Hirota, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R144" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R144">Olson, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>), while elevated (diabetic) glucose levels can reduce transepithelial resistance (<a href="#R64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R64">Garnett, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Barrier integrity in an <em>in vitro</em> mucosal culture model is essential to avoid accessibility of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> to the basolateral surface, especially given its preferential binding to basolateral receptors (<a href="#R20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R20">Bucior, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). When studying the effect of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence factors that affect barrier function (including ExoS, <em>N</em>-(3 oxododecanoyl)-<span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-homoserine lactone (3-oxo-C<sub>12</sub> HSL), rhamnolipids, and elastase (<a href="#R7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R7">Azghani, <em>et al.</em>, 1993</a>, <a href="#R203" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R203">Vikstrom, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R224" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R224">Zulianello, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R187" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R187">Soong, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R74">Halldorsson, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R209" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R209">Wallace, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>)), it is important to distinguish targeted disruption of cell-cell contacts from host cell death and subsequent disruption of epithelial integrity (<a href="#R224" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R224">Zulianello, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R187" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R187">Soong, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R53">Engel &amp; Eran, 2011</a>). Using <em>in vitro</em> models that do not express tight junctions or have diminished barrier function are relevant for diseases involving repairing epithelium, while intact barrier integrity characterizes mucosal tissues of a healthy host (<a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R64">Garnett, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>).</p></section><section id="S15"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Extracellular matrix proteins</h3>
<p id="P19">As mentioned earlier, breaching of the epithelial barrier through injury or disruption of tight junctions can expose the basolateral surface to <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. ECM proteins of the basement membrane, such as fibronectin, laminin and collagens, enable <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion and can contribute to colonization and dissemination (<a href="#R195" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R195">Trafny, <em>et al.</em>, 1995</a>, <a href="#R40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R40">de Bentzmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R156" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R156">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>, <a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Furthermore, cellular ECM components at the apical surface whose expression has been reported to be induced in repairing epithelium, bind <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R170">Roger, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>). Therefore, the presence and distribution of these ECM proteins in an <em>in vitro</em> model of infection is of interest. Not only can the ECM composition of the basement membrane (and potentially apical surface) affect <em>P. aeruginosa</em> colonization, cellular differentiation is also greatly dependent on ECM proteins (<a href="#R18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R18">Boudreau &amp; Bissell, 1998</a>, <a href="#R185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R185">Simon-Assmann, <em>et al.</em>, 1998</a>). By means of example, 16 HBE cells are not polarized and do not express functional CFTR when grown at an ALI on thin collagen I gels, while these same cells are polarized and express apical CFTR on thick collagen I gels or on a mixture of ECM proteins (<a href="#R33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R33">Cozens, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R160">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>) (<a href="#T2" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). ECM composition of the basement membrane in an <em>in vitro</em> cell culture model will vary depending on (i) the ECM composition of the substrate on which the cells are grown (e.g. collagen I for ALI), (ii) the cell type chosen, and (iii) the experimental conditions for generating the model. Specifically, observations of lung and intestinal epithelial cells grown on microcarrier beads in 3-D in the RWV bioreactor exhibited higher levels of basolateral collagen IV and laminin, compared to the same cells grown as 2-D monolayers (<a href="#R143" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R143">Nickerson, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R25">Carterson, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). The use of IVOC models in these applications is also of interest since the <em>in vivo</em> basement membrane is maintained in these model systems (<a href="#R196" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R196">Tsang, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R135">Middleton, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>). Taken together, it is of particular importance to choose an <em>in vitro</em> cell culture model that appropriately represents the cellular and basement membrane ECM composition when studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in the context of damaged/repairing epithelium, since enhanced binding to basolateral (and possibly apical) ECM components contributes to the infectious disease process.</p></section><section id="S16"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Mucus</h3>
<p id="P20">In the airways, mucins are either gel-like mucous secretions produced by goblet cells and submucosal glands (MUC5AC, MUC5B, MUC2) or associated with the membranes or cilia of respiratory epithelial cells (MUC1, MUC4 and MUC16) (<a href="#R92" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R92">Hovenberg, <em>et al.</em>, 1996</a>) (<a href="#R117" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R117">Li, <em>et al.</em>, 1997</a>, <a href="#R184" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R184">Sharma, <em>et al.</em>, 1998</a>, <a href="#R71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R71">Groneberg, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R183" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R183">Schulz, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Ciliated and non-ciliated epithelial cells produce membrane-associated mucins (<a href="#R23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R23">Buisine, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R78">Hattrup &amp; Gendler, 2008</a>, <a href="#R54" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R54">Evans &amp; Koo, 2009</a>, <a href="#R206" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R206">Voynow &amp; Rubin, 2009</a>). Model systems that contain a mucus layer include IVOCs and primary airway epithelium grown at an ALI (<a href="#R159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R159">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1991</a>, <a href="#R28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R28">Coakley, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R148" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R148">Paradiso, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R69">Gray, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>, <a href="#R176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R176">Sajjan, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>). In addition, RWV-derived 3-D culture models of several epithelial cell lines (A549, BEAS2-B, HT-29, Int-407) exhibit mucus production, including MUC5AC and MUC1, while the same cells grown as 2-D monolayers show a very low or absent mucus content (<a href="#R143" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R143">Nickerson, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R25">Carterson, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R91">Honer zu Bentrup, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R199" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R199">Vaughan, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R202" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R202">Vertrees, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>, <a href="#R164" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R164">Radtke, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). The absence or presence of this host-associated factor in an <em>in vitro</em> mucosal model can impact the outcome of a <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease study. The flagellum of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> binds carbohydrate structures of mucins (such as MUC1), which enhances <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion (<a href="#R24" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R24">Carnoy, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R5">Arora, <em>et al.</em>, 1998</a>, <a href="#R120" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R120">Lillehoj, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R119" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R119">Lillehoj, <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>, <a href="#R136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R136">Mitchell, <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>, <a href="#R124" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R124">Lu, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>). The presence of an apical mucus layer was shown to increase growth and/or adherence of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> compared to the same model in which the mucus layer was removed (<a href="#R215" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R215">Worlitzsch, <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>, <a href="#R64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R64">Garnett, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). These data further indicate the importance of mucus when studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> virulence traits.</p>
<p id="P21">It is important to note that the physicochemical properties and/or thickness of the mucus layer in the airways and other tissues may vary depending on the disease studied. The most common example is the lungs of CF patients, where epithelial cells are covered with a thick dehydrated mucus layer that contains higher levels of DNA and lower levels of the MUC5AC and MUC5B mucins compared to normal lungs (<a href="#R80" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R80">Henke, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>, <a href="#R172" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R172">Rubin, 2007</a>), even though concentrations of these mucins were not different during exacerbations (<a href="#R81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R81">Henke, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>). In addition, mucus glycosylation of both secreted and cell-associated mucins is affected in patients with CF and results in a unique glycosylation phenotype (reviewed by (<a href="#R200" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R200">Venkatakrishnan, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>)). A link between dysfunctional CFTR in the CF patient population and undersialyation of membrane-tethered but not secreted mucins has been suggested (<a href="#R11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R11">Barasch &amp; al-Awqati, 1993</a>, <a href="#R44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R44">Dosanjh, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>, <a href="#R109" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R109">Kube, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R200" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R200">Venkatakrishnan, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>), which reiterates the importance of a functional CFTR depending on the disease studied (see section on Apical-basolateral polarity). The CF mucus composition has been shown to drastically affect the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process. Specifically, normally hydrated mucus does not result in the formation of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> biofilms as opposed to CF-like concentrated mucus (<a href="#R131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R131">Matsui, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>). The unique CF glycosylation phenotype of mucins has been proposed to enhance <em>P. aeruginosa</em> adhesion to mucus, resulting in a diminished clearance (<a href="#R127" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R127">Martino, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R200" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R200">Venkatakrishnan, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>).</p>
<p id="P22">Since most ΔF508-CFTR mutant epithelial cell lines do not produce mucus or only limited amounts under currently tested culture conditions (<a href="#R137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R137">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>), the combined role of the airway epithelium and the distinct CF mucus composition has not been extensively studied. Therefore, developing <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models that mimic the differentiated CF host epithelium and the associated mucus layer are of interest to the field.</p></section><section id="S17"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Cilia</h3>
<p id="P23">Mimicking the <em>in vivo</em> mucociliary clearance is also of interest when choosing a model to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis, whether it is in the context of a healthy population or of an underlying disease. Herewith, it needs to be taken into consideration that certain diseases are characterized by dysfunctional cilia. In patients with CF, the impaired or absent mucociliary clearance is believed to be at least in part causative for the susceptibility to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections. In addition, other chronic obstructive pulmonary disorders, immotile ciliary disorders, and physical injury (e.g., insertion of tracheal tubes, suction catheters, bronchoscopes) can result in impaired or absent ciliary function, and these patients frequently suffer from acute or chronic <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infections (<a href="#R118" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R118">Lieberman, 2003</a>, <a href="#R174" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R174">Sagel, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Also, the ciliary beat frequency may vary depending on the location in the respiratory system (<a href="#R223" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R223">Zhao, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Therefore, the need for functional cilia in a lung tissue culture model largely depends on the disease studied and the experimental question being addressed.</p>
<p id="P24">While <em>P. aeruginosa</em> was shown not to adhere to ciliated epithelial cells (<a href="#R165" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R165">Ramphal &amp; Pyle, 1983</a>, <a href="#R159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R159">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1991</a>, <a href="#R214" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R214">Woodworth, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>), ciliary beat frequency might affect the adherence of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, with a higher beat frequency leading to lower adherence (<a href="#R223" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R223">Zhao, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). These findings highlight the importance of mimicking mucociliary clearance <em>in vitro</em>. Most publications describing the effect of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and its virulence factors (such as lectins, rhamnolipids, proteases and phenazines) on ciliary function have used primary respiratory epithelium grown as ALI or IVOCs (<a href="#R213" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R213">Wilson, <em>et al.</em>, 1985</a>, <a href="#R83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R83">Hingley, <em>et al.</em>, 1986</a>, <a href="#R84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R84">Hingley, <em>et al.</em>, 1986</a>, <a href="#R141" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R141">Munro, <em>et al.</em>, 1989</a>, <a href="#R166" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R166">Read, <em>et al.</em>, 1992</a>, <a href="#R9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R9">Bajolet-Laudinat, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>). Indeed, these models contain ciliated cells at ratios similar to the respiratory mucosa. However, since cilia are already severely damaged after 2 hours of exposure to <em>P. aeruginosa</em> in IVOCs (<a href="#R159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R159">Plotkowski, <em>et al.</em>, 1991</a>), it is challenging to assess the long-term role of cilia in the pathogenesis of this microorganism using currently available <em>in vivo</em>-like cell culture models. In this regard, it could be of interest to add antibiotics (such as bacitracin, clindamycin, gramicidin and roxithromycin) to the culture medium, since these agents were shown to partially counteract <em>P. aeruginosa</em>-induced effects on ciliary beat frequency (<a href="#R125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R125">Mallants, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>).</p></section><section id="S18"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Multicellular complexity – The host and its indigenous microbiota</h3>
<p id="P25">Most <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models used to date for studying the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process were comprised of one cell type, whether that is a single cell line or primary cells. However, interactions between multiple cell types are necessary to mediate antimicrobial defense and inflammatory reactions normally observed in mucosal tissues. Therefore, using a series of hierarchical models with increasing complexity can help dissect molecular mechanisms of pathogenesis, and replicate the <em>in vivo</em> infectious disease process more closely (<a href="#R178" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R178">Schmeichel &amp; Bissell, 2003</a>, <a href="#R70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R70">Griffith &amp; Swartz, 2006</a>, <a href="#R67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R67">Grandel, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>, <a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R36">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Two-dimensional monolayers of various epithelial cell lines typically result in a homogenous cell population of one cell type, while RWV-derived 3-D aggregates of these cell lines exhibit phenotypes of multiple cell types (<a href="#R12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R12">Barrila, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). For example, RWV-derived models of intestinal epithelium contained populations of enterocytes, M-like cells, Paneth cells and goblet cells, which represent four out of five major cell types in the <em>in vivo</em> parental tissue (<a href="#R143" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R143">Nickerson, <em>et al.</em>, 2001</a>, <a href="#R91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R91">Honer zu Bentrup, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>, <a href="#R164" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R164">Radtke, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). ALI cultures of primary respiratory epithelium generate basal, goblet and ciliated cells (<a href="#R224" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R224">Zulianello, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>), but do not recapitulate the more than forty epithelial cell types in the lung (<a href="#R169" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R169">Rock &amp; Hogan, 2011</a>). Therefore, the collective role of multiple cell types, including innate immune and other cell populations, and their interactions, are not considered using available <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models. IVOCs offer an interesting alternative since in theory all cells from the biopsy region are present in the model (<a href="#R135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R135">Middleton, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>). However, the limited lifespan of immune cells such as lymphocytes in these models are a limitation of IVOCs when attempting to replicate the <em>in vivo</em> multicellular complexity (<a href="#R56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R56">Farley, <em>et al.</em>, 1986</a>, <a href="#R62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R62">Forsgren, <em>et al.</em>, 1994</a>). Reconstituting the entire functional cell population of a parental tissue in an <em>in vitro</em> setting is challenging. Challenges include (i) finding an appropriate medium type that enables growth of all cell types simultaneously, (ii) obtaining relevant 3-D organization of all cell types relative to each other, (iii) maintaining viability and physiologically relevant ratios of each respective cell type, (iv) incorporating appropriate physical forces, and (v) incorporating the relevant ECM composition required to support <em>in vivo</em>-like function of tissue-like constructs. Therefore, existing model systems have co-cultured two to three different cell types, mostly epithelial cells with neutrophils, eosinophils, monocytes, macrophages, or lymphocytes, when investigating bacterial pathogenesis and host innate immunity in response to infection (reviewed by (<a href="#R49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R49">Duell, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>)). Inclusion of immune cells such as macrophages or neutrophils are relevant given their generation of reactive oxygen and nitrogen species, and cytokines, which affect <em>P. aeruginosa</em> viability and expression of clinically important virulence factors (<a href="#R130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R130">Mathee, <em>et al.</em>, 1999</a>, <a href="#R61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R61">Forman &amp; Torres, 2002</a>, <a href="#R216" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R216">Wu, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Limited available literature incorporated multiple cell types of mucosal tissues in an <em>in vitro</em> model to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis. A RWV-derived 3-D co-culture model of alveolar epithelial cells and functional macrophages demonstrated that epithelial cells could protect macrophages from the cytotoxic effects of the quorum sensing molecule 3-oxo-C<sub>12</sub> HSL (<a href="#R36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R36">Crabbé, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>), presumably by enzymatic degradation (<a href="#R27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R27">Chun, <em>et al.</em>, 2004</a>, <a href="#R146" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R146">Ozer, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). A recent study by Tang <em>et al.</em> showed that co-culturing lung epithelial cells with wild type (but not CFTR knockout) lymphocytes affects bicarbonate secretion by CFTR, which in turn enhances the killing of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a href="#R192" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R192">Tang, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Using a Transwell model of wild type or ΔF508-CFTR primary airway cells, Farberman <em>et al</em>. showed that <em>P. aeruginosa</em>-induced expression of CXC chemokines could attract neutrophils from the basolateral to apical surface more efficiently in CF cells (<a href="#R55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R55">Farberman, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). While the full potential of co-culture models remains to be explored, these studies highlight that mimicking the multicellular complexity of the <em>in vivo</em> parental tissue can provide novel insights in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis, and can help dissect the role of each cell type and their interactions in the outcome of disease.</p>
<p id="P26">Multicellular complexity of mucosal tissues also encompasses the thousands of microbial species that colonize the mucosal epithelium in health and disease. With the reciprocal interactions between the host and microbiome, and their key role in innate immunity, it has become increasingly clear that the indigenous microbiota are important in the infectious disease process (<a href="#R26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R26">Chu &amp; Mazmanian, 2013</a>). Since the microbiota is involved in tissue differentiation and function, such as epithelial barrier function, mucus production, innate immune cell activation (<a href="#R100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R100">Kau, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>, <a href="#R75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R75">Han, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R90">Holmes, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R123">Lozupone, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>, <a href="#R52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R52">Eloe-Fadrosh &amp; Rasko, 2013</a>), incorporating microbial communities when modeling <em>in vivo P. aeruginosa</em> infections is a logical consideration. As with building the host multicellular complexity, challenges arise with developing <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models that mimic the <em>in vivo</em> microbiome. These include the 70% as of yet unculturable bacteria in the human body, creating the right environment to maintain relevant ratios of community members (without having the faster growing organisms taking over the community), and maintaining host viability in the presence of large numbers of oxygen/nutrient-consuming organisms. To date, limited studies have incorporated host-associated microbial consortia in <em>in vitro</em> models, but we anticipate that future developments will further unveil the importance of the microbiome in host-pathogen interactions by <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. <em>In vitro</em> models of the microbiome are of particular importance since findings from animal models do not always translate to humans (<a href="#R100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R100">Kau, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).</p></section><section id="S19"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Other host factors altered during disease</h3>
<p id="P27">In this section, additional examples of disease-associated changes in the mucosal epithelium microenvironment that were found to alter the <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infectious disease process using <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models are discussed, i.e. iron, oxygen, pH and nutrients. <strong>Iron</strong> levels are increased in the CF lung environment (reviewed by (<a href="#R167" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R167">Reid, <em>et al.</em>, 2007</a>)), which is reflected in ΔF508-CFTR bronchial epithelial cells (<a href="#R137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R137">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>). These enhanced iron levels resulted in higher host-associated <em>P. aeruginosa</em> biofilm formation compared to wild type CFTR cells (<a href="#R137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R137">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>), which could be prevented by using a combination of iron chelators and tobramycin (<a href="#R138" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R138">Moreau-Marquis, <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). Another host-associated factor that plays a role in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis is <strong>hypoxia</strong>. Hypoxic growth conditions, associated with surgical injury in the intestinal tract, triggers production of the PA-I lectin in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> by soluble and contact-mediated factors released by intestinal epithelial cells (<a href="#R107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R107">Kohler, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Similarly, hypoxic conditions in the context of CF lung disease were suggested to affect internalization of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> into different lung epithelial cell lines (<a href="#R177" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R177">Schaible, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). However, <a href="#R177" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R177">Schaible <em>et al</em>.</a> used non-polarized 2-D monolayers of lung epithelial cells that do not form tight junctions. Since reduced oxygen levels increase tight junction formation and polarization, this could have affected <em>P. aeruginosa</em> internalization (see section on Junctional complex) (<a href="#R107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R107">Kohler, <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>, <a href="#R85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R85">Hirota, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R144" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R144">Olson, <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). The <strong>pH</strong> of CF mucus has been shown to be lower than normal secretions (<a href="#R28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R28">Coakley, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R186" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R186">Song, <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>), and could be correlated to differences in bicarbonate secretion by the epithelial cells (<a href="#R148" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R148">Paradiso, <em>et al.</em>, 2003</a>, <a href="#R163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R163">Quinton, 2010</a>). Lower pH levels in airway surface liquid reduced <em>P. aeruginosa</em> killing, presumably through inactivation of pH-sensitive antimicrobials (<a href="#R151" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R151">Pezzulo, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Finally, <strong>nutritional factors</strong> such as elevated basolateral glucose (CF-related diabetes) was shown to stimulate apical growth of <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, and this effect was more pronounced in CF airway epithelial cells (<a href="#R64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R64">Garnett, <em>et al.</em>, 2013</a>). Additional host microenvironmental factors in mucosal tissues, such as changes in viscoelastic stretch forces during lung disease, are of interest to replicate in <em>in vitro</em> cell/tissue models when studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis and have to our knowledge not yet been addressed.</p></section></section><section id="S20"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Perspectives</h2>
<p id="P28"><em>In vitro</em> cell culture models have layed the foundation for various novel antimicrobial approaches, of which some were tested in the clinic (<a href="#R108" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R108">Kohler, <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>, <a href="#R88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R88">Hoiby, 2011</a>, <a href="#R43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="R43">Doring, <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). However, most of these new approaches against <em>P. aeruginosa</em> have failed during clinical trials, hereby reiterating the complexity of host physiology in health and disease. This reality emphasizes the need to develop and focus research efforts towards more differentiated physiologically relevant cell culture models. Appropriately representing the human host in a cell culture model is essential to capture complex reciprocal and dynamic interactions between the host, its microenvironment, and the pathogen, which govern the outcome of the infection process. With regard to <em>in vitro</em> cell culture models for studying <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis, areas that need improvement include (i) enhancing host cell viability upon <em>P. aeruginosa</em> infection (especially in the context of chronic biofilm and adaptation studies), (ii) increasing the multicellular complexity (including immune cells and members of the indigenous microbiota), (iii) incorporating relevant ECM proteins, (iv) reconciling paracrine, endocrine and cellular factors, and (v) integrating physical forces to mimic multi-organ signaling and interactions that naturally occur within the context of the specific tissue microenvironment of interest. On the other hand, researchers currently have state-of-the-art, simplified models available that mimic the minimal functional unit of the differentiated tissue <em>in vivo</em> and remain to be fully explored for <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis and drug design. In this regard, detailed knowledge of the <em>in vivo</em> characteristics of a given <em>in vitro</em> model is key to understanding whether a specific model system is suitable to answer the experimental question being asked, and to accurately understand the data gathered and extrapolate these to the <em>in vivo</em> disease process. With our continuously expanding understanding of the host microenvironmental factors that contribute to disease by <em>P. aeruginosa</em>, and our knowledge database and bioengineering abilities, <em>in vitro</em> model systems can and will continue to be improved to reconcile more physiological traits. Hence, antimicrobial therapies can be designed that are effective against the host-associated <em>P. aeruginosa</em> phenotype, and function in the unique, and sometimes chemically challenging <em>in vivo</em> conditions. Applying these valuable <em>in vitro</em> platforms to study <em>P. aeruginosa</em> pathogenesis will increase the translation of research findings from bench to clinic.</p></section><section id="S21" class="ack"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Acknowledgements</h2>
<p id="P29">We apologize to all the researchers whose papers could not be cited in this review due to space limitations. This work was supported by NASA grants NNX10AO52G and NNX13AM01G, and by NIH grant 1 RC4 HL 106625.</p></section><section id="ref-list1" class="ref-list"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">References</h2>
